P R E FA C E
T
his book shows you how to build a tradeable strategy. A tradeable strat-
egy is one that fits your own risk/reward goals, and one that trades in 
realâ€time as well as it performs in the development backâ€test. It is not easy 
to develop a tradeable strategy because there are serious pitfalls. Most of us 
are greedy and want to trade something that has a large return. We ratio-
nalize that we can accept a lot of risk in the form of drawâ€down to achieve 
the large return, but in reality few traders can trade through a 20 percent 
drawâ€down before abandoning the strategy. To help you form realistic risk/
reward goals, the first chapter shows the realâ€time performance of the best 
traders in the world over a recent fiveâ€year period. Based on that chapter, I 
urge you to write down the system characteristics that would be tradeable 
for you. Address risk first in the form of max drawâ€down, average annual 
max drawâ€down, and longest time between equity highs. So the first pitfall 
is greed. It manifests itself in every strategy development step. You will be 
inclined to accept a system rule that increases profitability, even though it 
increases risk at a higher rate. But an even greater pitfall is the danger of 
curveâ€fitting.
Curveâ€fitting occurs when you develop a strategy with too few trades in 
the development sample. Curveâ€fit systems impact the second characteristic 
of a tradeable system: Curveâ€fit systems donâ€™t trade in realâ€time as well as 
they backâ€test. It is unfortunate that the system development packages you 
can buy are almost universally tied to the oneâ€chart paradigm; you place 
many bars of data from a single tradeable on a chart and develop a strategy 
to trade that instrument. Even if your developed strategy yields hundreds of 

viii
PREFACE 
trades, it probably isnâ€™t enough to be anything other than heavily curveâ€fit. 
Chapter 2 covers curveâ€fitting extensively and shows how you can generate 
enough trades to minimize curveâ€fitting. It uses examples and statistics, and 
details an easyâ€toâ€perform process that you can use to determine the degree 
of curveâ€fitting in your development work. Most strategies fail or underâ€
perform because they are overly curveâ€fit. If you want to succeed, you need 
to fully understand how to minimize curveâ€fitting.
Most of the remainder of the book is spent developing two tradeable 
systems: one a shortâ€term scalping system for stocks, and the other a 
midâ€term trendâ€following strategy for commodities. As these systems 
are developed, entries, exits, and trading filters are detailed. By the end 
of the development process, both are â€œtradeableâ€ as is, but how to tai-
lor them to a range of risk/reward profiles is covered in, five chapters 
on money management. I make a differentiation between small-account 
traders and large-account traders. Small-account traders are always a 
handful of adverse trades away from a margin call. They cannot take 
advantage of sizing techniques that optimize equity growth because the 
risk of more than a oneâ€lot might be 10 or even 20 percent of their ac-
count size. Using smallâ€account trading techniques, money management 
rules for both the stock and commodity systems are detailed in two 
chapters. Similarly, the systems are adapted for the largeâ€account trader 
in two chapters. Lastly, both systems are combined. Table P.1 illustrates 
the range of tradeable solutions available.

he purpose of this book is to show the reader how to develop a trade-

Itâ€™s nice to have lofty goals, but youâ€™ll never find a strategy that meets those

strategyâ€™s performance. Lastly, a set of questions will be presented to help

 These performance numbers probably introduce a reality shock into 
those looking to develop their own tradeable strategy. Real trading involves 
these tenets:
 
â–  You are not going to make 100 percent each year. The number-one CTA 
in the list averaged 44.54 percent for 5 years, but even then it had a 
12â€month period that netted only 1 percent. 
 
â–  If you shoot for a relatively high return, you will have a relatively high 
drawâ€down at some point. If you shoot for a relatively lower return, you 
should be able to see relatively less drawâ€down. 
 
â–    If you are fortunate enough to have a period of exceptional returns, 
remember that those gains may have to carry your performance for 
years. 
 
â–  You will go extended periods of time without any gains.

â– Metrics to Use in Gauging Tradeable  
System Performance
Based on the performance of some of the best money managers in the world, 
there are a minimum of three areas that need to be addressed in defining a 
suitable tradeable system:
 1. Some measure of normal return and normal drawâ€down. These are 
the risk/reward benchmarks youâ€™ll be experiencing most of the 
time.
 2. The worstâ€case risk benchmark. This is the worst drawâ€down youâ€™ll 
have to trade through over an extended period of timeâ€”maybe the 
worst case in the last 5 or 10 years.
 3. The longest flat time. This is the longest time youâ€™ll go before hitting a 
new equity high.
Looking at worstâ€case risk first, weâ€™ve seen that the best managers in the 
world normally average just a little more than their fiveâ€year biggest drawâ€
down. A goal of a tradeable system is one that averages more per year than 
its largest drawâ€down. This book will show that, in general, return can be 
increased or decreased through leverage. As you increase or decrease the re-
turn, your drawâ€down will also go up or down. It makes sense then to define 
the max drawâ€down you are willing to accept and use leverage to bring the 
system performance down to a point where the max drawâ€down is less than 
your acceptable threshold.
Itâ€™s my experience that itâ€™s hard to design a system with minimal â€œflat 
timeâ€ as a design metric. You do the best you can through the development 
process and when youâ€™ve got something that meets your riskâ€reward goals, 
you see what the other metrics look like. If flat time, or some other charac-
teristic, is way over what youâ€™re willing to trade through, the best way to fix 
the problem is to introduce another type of strategy that is lowly correlated 
to the one with the issue.
 
â– Know Yourself
Itâ€™s easy to start the design process and say, â€œI want something that has a max 
drawâ€down of 20 percent, and for that risk Iâ€™ll accept an average return of 
25 percent.â€  The problem starts when youâ€™ve developed such a strategy and 

5
WHAT IS A TRADEABLE STRATEGY?
start to trade. Suppose you go three months and are down 10 percent. I 
donâ€™t care who you are, youâ€™ll start to have doubts:
 
â– Is the strategy curveâ€fit in some way?
 
â– Has the market changed?
 
â– Volatility seems too high (too low). Is that the problem?
If youâ€™ve set your max drawâ€down too high, by the time you recover, or 
get to 20 percent, youâ€™ll be a wreck. This is where it becomes important to 
know yourself. If youâ€™re new to trading and never experienced a 20â€percent 
drawâ€down, shoot for lessâ€”probably much less. Iâ€™ve talked to thousands of 
traders, and most say, â€œI can weather a 20 percent drawâ€down if the return is 
X percent,â€ but in reality few can go that far. Donâ€™t look at drawâ€down as a 
function of return. Youâ€™ve got to get through the drawâ€downs to realize the 
return, so set your max drawâ€down at a level you know you can trade through.
 
â– Conclusion
The best money managers in the world average less than 30 percent a year, and 
experience a max drawâ€down every five years that is only a few percent less than 
their average return. Unfortunately, statistics on average annual max drawâ€down 
arenâ€™t easy to find, but that is an important number because thatâ€™s what you need 
to trade through every year to achieve your return. Our goals then for develop-
ing a tradeable system must include these two performance objectives:
 1. Average annual return is greater than max drawâ€down over at least a 
fiveâ€year period.
 2. Average annual return needs to be a multiple of the average annual max 
drawâ€down.
The third required performance objective is defined by the trader: What 
is the max drawâ€down you will tolerate? Pick a realistic number.
This book will show you how to develop strategies that meet these 
objectives. In fact, when weâ€™re done, there will be fully defined stock 
and commodity strategies that you can trade as-is if you wish. Before we 
develop these systems, thereâ€™s really another part to tradeability and that 
is, your strategy must trade in real time like it does on your developmen-
tal data. The next chapter will discuss how to develop your strategy so it 
trades like it backâ€tests.

D
eveloping a tradeable strategy involves two steps. The first is to define 
the risk/reward balance the trader can live with, and the second is to 
develop a strategy that meets those guidelines and trades in real time like it 
backâ€tests. Most of the rest of this book will address how to develop strate-
gies that meet your trading objectives. This chapter addresses something much 
more important: the pitfalls a developer will run into in developing a strategy 
that will trade like it backtests. There are many â€œhow to developâ€ ways; this 
book only shows mine, but there is only one way to make sure your developed 
strategy trades in real time like it backtests. That one way involves minimizing 
curveâ€fitting in your development and making trading assumptions in your 
development that work as realistically as possible. Far and away, the biggest 
pitfall is curveâ€fitting. This chapter uses statistics to help clarify the concept, 
but donâ€™t skip over it because of the math. These are important concepts, and 
if you donâ€™t understand them you will continually develop systems that fail in 
realâ€time trading and you will never understand why.

DEVELOPING A STRATEGY SO IT TRADES LIKE IT BACKâ€TESTS
 
â– Curve-Fitting
When youâ€™re developing a trading strategy, you test trading ideas against 
historical data to see if the idea is profitable. If you had an infinite amount 
of historical data, you could be sure that an idea that proves profitable on 
the historical data would be just as profitable in the future. But we never  
have an infinite amount of data; we only have a few decades worth of data in 
the best case. With that small subset of the â€œinfinite data pool,â€ we canâ€™t be 
sure a backtested profitable idea will continue to be profitable in the future. 
This development problem is often exacerbated by a practice called curveâ€
fitting. Curveâ€fitting can be defined in the following way: either the overuse 
of trading rules, parameters, filters, stops, and so forth when developing a 
trading strategy on a relatively large body of data, or the proper use of rules, 
filters, and so on on a relatively small body of data.
A curveâ€fitting example will be given for each of these cases. For the case 
where a relatively large body of data was used, hereâ€™s a true story. I knew 
a trader in the late â€™90s that developed an S&P 500 futures trading system 
using 45â€minute bars. He had S&P 45â€minute bar data spanning the 1984â€“
1998 time frame. During most of that time, the S&P 500 futures opened at 
9:30 EST and closed at 16:15 EST, so there were nine 45â€minute bars each 
day for the 15 years. Thatâ€™s a total of about 34,000 bars of historical data. 
With all that data, he developed a trading system that made less than 90 
trades in the 15 years. Thatâ€™s a trade every other month. But those 90 trades 
were spectacular. There were only a few losers, and the average trade was 
over $2,000. He figured he could double a starting amount of $10,000 each 
year with the strategy. I saw him a few years later and asked how his system 
was working out. He scratched his head and said he couldnâ€™t figure out what 
went wrong. The system â€œstopped workingâ€ as soon as he started trading 
it. I believe that what really happened is that he used so many rules, filters, 
and so forth on the relatively large amount of data that he isolated 90 short 
periods of time that were highly profitable.
Curveâ€Fitting Using Good Development  
Practices on a Small Amount of Data
Curveâ€fitting on a relatively small body of data can be illustrated by looking 
at the Swiss franc during the 1980s. That timeâ€frame was picked because 
the Swiss franc trended beautifully in the 1980s. Letâ€™s use a moving average 
to determine the trend. If todayâ€™s nâ€day average is higher than yesterdayâ€™s,

position and go short. Itâ€™s called a reversal system. You always have a posi-
tion and that position alternates from long to short to long. The breakout in

This table illustrates how well the Swiss franc trended in the 80s. There is 
substantial profit at each moving average point. Letâ€™s select the 50â€day mov-
ing average point and try to improve results by adding an nâ€day lookâ€back 
filter. One of the tenets of trend trading is to only trade in the direction 
of the longâ€term trend. An nâ€day lookâ€back filter is a way to do that. You 
look back n days and if the close today is higher than that close, the trend 
is up and you only take long trades. In looking back nâ€days, if todayâ€™s close 
is less than the nâ€day lookâ€back close, you only take short trades. When we 
add that logic to our 50â€day reversal system, the results in Table 2.2 are for 
lookâ€backs ranging from 60 days to 150 days.
Letâ€™s select the 110â€day lookâ€back filter as a good addition to the system, 
and now look to add some risk control. Letâ€™s use a â€œcatastrophic stop lossâ€ 
to get us out of trades that move â€œtoo farâ€ into loss. When we enter a long 
or short position, the catastrophic stop is placed X dollars away from the 
entry. If that stop is hit, we donâ€™t reenter in the same direction until an 
entry in the other direction is signaled. There are two ways to implement 
this stop. First you can enter the stop as a â€œgood until canceledâ€ stop loss. 

10
DEVELOPING A STRATEGY SO IT TRADES LIKE IT BACKâ€TESTS
In that case, the stop is always in the market (just when the marketâ€™s open; 
nothing trades 24/7). The second way is to not place the stop into the mar-
ket, but instead wait until there is a close adverse to the stop and exit at the 
next open. In that case you can have losses well in excess of your stop loss, 
but there are two benefits. One benefit is that the stop is not active during 
periods of illiquidity; almost every commodity has bid/ask spreads that 
increase overnight, sometimes dramatically, and with an active stop thereâ€™s 
a chance of a horrendous fill. The other benefit is that many times an active 
stop would be hit during the day, but the market reverses from that point 
to close inside the stop. In that case your trade is still alive to turn profit-
able. The second stop method is used to generate the results in Table 2.3.

 Curveâ€Fitting Is Proportional to the Number of 
Trades in the Development Sample 
 If you compare the two curveâ€fi tting examples, thereâ€™s one similarity: They 
both had a relatively small number of trades in the development sample. 
Letâ€™s postulate that curveâ€fi tting is proportional to the number of trades 
in the development sample. We know that if we had an infi nite number of 
trades, weâ€™d have the entire universe of trades and there could be no curveâ€
fi tting. The question is: How many trades are â€œclose enoughâ€ to represent 
the infi nite sample? An experiment we can use to get an insight into that 
question is to take a commodity and generate a large number of trades, 
then sample from the large number and see how many trades need to be in 
the smaller sample to generate a small diff erence from the â€œinfi niteâ€ sample 
statistics. If the hypothesis is correct, we should see the diff erence in results 
between the â€œinfi niteâ€ sample and our smaller sample sizes get smaller and 
smaller as the number of trades in the sample size increases. Figure   2.1   
shows the thought.
 FIGURE  2.1  Increasing Sample Size Reduces the Diff erence in Results from the 
Infi nite Sample 
Sample Size
Difference in Results
 The only issue with that methodology is generating â€œdiff erence in results.â€ 
Fortunately statisticians have solved this problem for us. Their methodology 
is called standard error. You compute the mean value of the infi nite sample 
(the average profi tâ€perâ€trade), and then randomly pick nâ€trade samples a 
number of times. If you compute the average of each of these samples, and 

13
DEVELOPING A STRATEGY SO IT TRADES LIKE IT BACKâ€TESTS
then compute the standard deviation of the averages, you have the standard 
error. Hopefully, an example will clarify things. 
 Suppose you have an infi nite number of trades generated by a strategy, 
and that the average profi t-per-trade across the infi nite sample was $100. 
You decide to randomly pull a number of 10â€trade samples and see how 
closely the results match the infi nite distribution. Figure  2.2 represents the 
results of randomly selecting fi ve 10â€trade samples.
 The average profi tâ€perâ€trade for each sample is:
 Sample 1 mean: 97.94 
 Sample 2 mean: 104.6 
 Sample 3 mean: 100.17 
 Sample 4 mean: 101.43 
 Sample 5 mean: 98.89 
 The standard deviation across those fi ve average profi tâ€perâ€trade values 
is 2.59; thatâ€™s also the standard error. Now for what it means. One standard 
deviation above and below the infi nite sample mean of 100 will statistically 
include 68.3 percent of the sample means. So 0.683 multiplied by 5 sample 
means is a value of 4.098 cases. That number of the sample means should be 
inside the 97.41 to 102.59 range (mean of 100 minus 1 standard deviation 
of 2.59 equals 97.41, and mean of 100 plus 2.59 equals 102.59). In our

case, four of the sample means were inside the range, and one (104.6) was 
outside. If we extended this case to draw hundreds of 10â€trade samples:
 
â– 68.3 percent of the sample means would fall in the range of 100 minus 1 
standard deviation to 100 plus 1 standard deviation.
 
â– 95.4 percent of the sample means would fall in the range of 100 minus 2 
standard deviations to 100 plus 2 standard deviations.
 
â– 99.7 percent of the sample means would fall in the range of 100 minus 3 
standard deviations to 100 plus 3 standard deviations.
As a trader, this means that if you developed a strategy that had an aver-
age profitâ€perâ€trade of $100 and a standard error of $2.59, you could be  
68.3 percent confident that the true profitâ€per-trade of the strategy was 
between $97.41 and $102.59. And you could be 99.7 percent confident that 
the true profit-per-trade was between $92.23 and $107.77. (My statistics 
professor would kill me for stating it that way, but dropping all the statistics 
caveats, thatâ€™s basically what it means.)
Thatâ€™s how standard error works, but in the real world, you donâ€™t get 
hundreds of nâ€trade samples. You only get one sample, and thatâ€™s your backâ€
test results. What do you do then? Well, thereâ€™s a way to get an estimate of 
the standard error of the mean using just that one sample. Compute the 
standard deviation of the trades in your backâ€test and divide by the square 
root of the sample size. Here are the results for our five samples of 10 trades:
Sample 1: mean = 97.94, standard deviation = 10.26, standard error  
= (10.26/âˆš10) = 3.25
Sample 2: mean = 104.6, standard deviation = 9.28, standard error  
= 2.94
Sample 3: mean = 100.17, standard deviation = 11.19, standard error 
= 3.54
Sample 4: mean = 101.43, standard deviation = 11.34, standard error 
= 3.59
Sample 5: mean = 98.89, standard deviation = 7.97, standard error = 2.52
The standard error should be 2.59, as we saw when we used all five of the 
samples. So the standard error results using just one sample donâ€™t match up 
exactly, but each one is close.
Before we leave hypothetical examples and move to trades, one other 
concept needs to be explored: the variance of the infinite sample. In the 
previous example, our five 10â€trade samples lined up pretty well with our 

15
DEVELOPING A STRATEGY SO IT TRADES LIKE IT BACKâ€TESTS
infinite trade mean of 100. The distance of each sample from the mean of 
100 wasnâ€™t very far. The reason they did is because a standard deviation of 
10 was chosen for the infinite sample when the thousands of trades that 
formed the infinite trade distribution were generated. Using a standard 
deviation of 10 means that 68.3 of the randomly generated trades will 
range from 90 to 110 (100 minus 1 standard deviation to 100 plus 1 stan-
dard deviation); 95.4 percent of the randomly generated trades will range 
from 80 to 120 (100 minus 2 standard deviations to 100 plus 2 standard 
deviations); and 99.7 percent of the trades will range from 70 to 130  
(100 minus 3 standard deviations to 100 plus 3 standard deviations). Real 
trade distributions are never this tight. Losers might be hundreds or even 
thousands of dollars, while winners can range the same distance, or more, 
to the plus side. Instead of the three-standard-deviation range of $60 we 
had with our example, real trade distributions can have ranges of many 
thousands of dollars. The bottom line is that the bigger the standard de-
viation (or variance) of the underlying distribution, the bigger the stan-
dard error. The following example parallels the previous example, but 
instead of a standard deviation of 10 for the infinite sample, weâ€™ll use 
a value of 500. That gives us a three-standard-deviation range of 3,000; 
99.7 percent of the trades will be between â€$1,500 and +$1,500 from 
the average profitâ€perâ€trade, or trade mean. Here are the results of pulling 
five 10â€trade samples from the â€œinfinite trade poolâ€ whose statistics are a 
trade mean of $100 and a trade standard deviation of $500.
Sample 1: mean = $263.29, standard error = $165.81
Sample 2: mean = âˆ’$31.05, standard error = $151.96
Sample 3: mean = âˆ’$71.57, standard error = $95.18
Sample 4: mean = $198.69, standard error = $148.14
Sample 5: mean = $275.74, standard error = $195.65
Average mean = $127.02
Based on the average mean of $127.02, this set of samples turned out 
more positive than the $100 infinite sample mean. The results show the ex-
panded standard error when the infinite distribution has a relatively large 
variance; instead of a standard error of 2.59 when the standard deviation has 
10, the standard error now ranges from $95.18 to $195.65 using a standard 
deviation of $500.
Again, what this means to the trader is that if his or her backâ€test yielded 
10 trades that averaged a profit of $275.74 with a standard error of $195.65 
(Sample 5), he or she could be 68.3 percent confident that the true average 

16
DEVELOPING A STRATEGY SO IT TRADES LIKE IT BACKâ€TESTS
profit-per-trade of the strategy was between $80.09 and $471.39. Maybe 
you would be willing to roll the dice with those odds and trade the strategy, 
but consider this: You could just as easily have yielded Sample 2 in your 
backâ€test. In that case your 68.3 percent chance sample mean would lie 
between âˆ’$183.01 and $120.91. Not attractive at all.
This example can be used to highlight another problem weâ€™re all sub-
ject to. Think of each of the five samples as a separate backâ€test, maybe 
with one parameter value changed slightly per run. Weâ€™re likely to think 
weâ€™re on the right track with and Samples 1 and 5, and on the wrong 
track with Samples 2 and 3, but the results come from the same distribu-
tion. We just canâ€™t make a judgment on a small sample with a relatively 
large standard deviation.
Testing the Hypothesis that Curveâ€Fitting Is Pro-
portional to the Number of  Trades in the Sample 
Using Real Trade Data
After that detour, letâ€™s get back to our hypothesis testing with real trade 
data. Gold was selected because I have daily data going back to 1975. To 
generate a lot of trades, a 20â€day moving average reversal system was used. 
It goes long when the average of the last 20 closes is higher than yesterdayâ€™s 
average, and closes the long position and goes short when todayâ€™s average of 
the last 20 closes is less than yesterdayâ€™s. The following are some the backâ€
test result statistics:
Winning trades: 233
Losing trades: 450
Total profit: $22,400
Average trade: $32.80
Largest winning trade: $16,230
Largest losing trade: $5,150
Standard deviation of the trades: $1,884
For this analysis, itâ€™s not important whether this is a tradeable strategy 
or not. All we want to know is how many trades we need in a sample to 
approximate the entire populationâ€™s results. For the analysis, the profit 
or loss value for each of the 683 gold trades was placed in a file. Then 
for each sample size the appropriate number of trades was randomly 
selected and the mean of that sample computed. This was done 10,000 
times for each sample size. The standard deviation of the 10,000 sample 

17
DEVELOPING A STRATEGY SO IT TRADES LIKE IT BACKâ€TESTS
means was used to compute the standard error for that sample size. To 
summarize for a sample size of 90 trades: 10,000 times 90 trades were 
randomly selected from the gold trade file containing the 683 trades; 
the average of the 90 trades for each of the 10,000 sets of trades was 
computed; the standard deviation of the 10,000 mean values was com-
puted; and then the standard error was computed by dividing the stan-
dard error by the square root of 90. The answer for that computation 
was about $200. Figure   2.3   shows the standard error versus the number 
of trades randomly selected for each sample. Sample size was limited to 
300 because there were only 683 trades in our trade file.

 For a trader there is a practical lesson that can be learned from the fore-

your sample size needs to be to have confi dence that the results are a true 
indication of how the strategy will trade in the future. In the fi rst example, 
the standard deviation of the trade sample was 10. That yielded a standard

error of about 2.5 when we pulled 10 samples from the distribution. When 
the standard deviation of the trade sample was increased to 500 and 10 
samples were used, the standard error jumped to a range of about 95 to 195. 
And from the last graph (Figure 2.4), we see that the standard error is about 
500 when the standard deviation of the trades grew to 2,316. Thereâ€™s good 
news and bad news about these facts for both shorterâ€term and longerâ€term 
traders:
 
â– Shorterâ€term strategies will have smaller average profits (bad news) but 
smaller average losses (good news). The standard deviation across all 
trades will be relatively smaller, so the standard error will be relatively 
smaller and fewer samples will be needed to minimize curveâ€fitting 
(good news).
 
â– Longerâ€term strategies will have larger average profits (good news) but 
larger average losses (bad news). The standard deviation across all trades 
will be relatively larger, so the standard error will be relatively larger and 
more samples will be needed to minimize curveâ€fitting (bad news).
 
â– Testing for Curve-Fitting
The statistics are over. The point of the previous section was that for most 
trading systems (I wonâ€™t say all, but Iâ€™m thinking all) you need many 
hundreds to thousands of trades in your backâ€test to minimize the effects 
of curveâ€fitting and gain the confidence that real trading will match the pa-
rameters of your backâ€test. I donâ€™t expect someone to statistically test his 
model to see if itâ€™s curveâ€fit. I have a relatively easy and robust way for you 
to convince yourself whether a system you develop is minimally curveâ€fit, 
or not. Itâ€™s called â€œBuild, Rebuild, and Compare,â€ or BRAC. The method in-
volves using all the historical data for the instrument(s) you intend to trade 
in your development sample. As you build the strategy, keep track of the 
steps you took and the selection logic you used, and if you wind up develop-
ing a strategy that performs well, youâ€™ve completed the â€œBuildâ€ part. Next, 
cut some data off the end of your historical data. If you were using 20 years 
of dailyâ€bar data, you might cut the last year off. Then with the smaller his-
torical database â€œRebuildâ€ your strategy using the same steps and decision 
criteria you used the first time. The last step is to input all the historical data 
into the â€œRebuiltâ€ strategy and â€œCompareâ€ the performance over the period 
of the withheld data with the performance of the first strategy over the same 

20
DEVELOPING A STRATEGY SO IT TRADES LIKE IT BACKâ€TESTS
period. If the performance is similar, you can have pretty good confidence 
that your strategy will hold up in real trading.

example, we used the following steps and used the accompanying selection 
logic:
 
â– Vary the number of days for the moving average from 10 to 120 in 10â€day 
increments. Select the number of days that yields the largest profit.
 
â– Vary the number of days for the lookâ€back filter from the moving aver-
age value plus 10 days to 200 in 10â€day increments. Select the lookâ€back 
value with the largest profit.
 
â– Vary the catastrophic stop value from $250 to $2,000 in $250 incre-
ments. Select the stop value that yields the largest profit.

BRAC versus Outâ€ofâ€Sample Testing
Itâ€™s funny how some things somehow get elevated to cult status as the beâ€all 
and endâ€all solution to a given problem. In the trading world, I consistently 
run across three things that I would put in that category:
 1. Monte Carlo analysis will tell you how much your strategy will really 
make, and what the worst drawâ€down will really be.
 2. A divergence signal is a very powerful entry technique.
 3. The only trustworthy measure of a good system is outâ€ofâ€sample testing.
I present my thoughts and analysis on Monte Carlo analysis and diver-
gence elsewhere in this book, but this is an appropriate time to talk about 
outâ€ofâ€sample testing. One of the questions asked most at seminars I present 
is: How much outâ€ofâ€sample testing do you do before you trade a system? 
I donâ€™t do any. If a developed system passes the BRAC test and it fits with 
the other systems Iâ€™m trading, Iâ€™ll trade it. The main problem with outâ€ 
ofâ€sample testing is there is no performance reference for the outâ€ofâ€sample 
results. Suppose you develop a system on all the available data and your 
outâ€ofâ€sample test is â€œwatch how it trades for the next year.â€  Your 20â€year 
backâ€test, which includes thousands of trades, averages $20,000 per year 
with an average drawâ€down of $10,000 a year and a max drawâ€down of 
$20,000. When you watch it for a year out of sample, you lose $5,000 and 
have a $15,000 drawâ€down along the way. Is this a failed outâ€ofâ€sample test? 
I think it depends. Suppose the last five years of trading looked like this:

If your outâ€ofâ€sample year had been four years ago, the $5,000 loss and 
$15,000 drawâ€down would have been the â€œrightâ€ answer, but based on this 
test most people would discard the strategy.
If your outâ€ofâ€sample methodology is to develop the strategy on 19 out 
of 20 years and then see how it does when you add back in the missing 
year, you are missing critical data. A shortfall of data is a huge development 
problem. If we had infinite data, this chapter wouldnâ€™t exist. Why make the 
problem even worse by withholding it?
The BRAC methodology solves both of these problems by giving you a 
performance reference for data not included in the development sample, 
and by letting you use all available data for your development sample at the 
same time.
Last Word on Curveâ€Fitting
I think thereâ€™s a conspiracy out there that leads traders into the curveâ€fitting 
trap. The conspiracy is perpetrated by companies that sell development soft-
ware. Almost every package has the â€œchart paradigmâ€ where you put one, or 
more, charts in a window and develop a strategy on the data for the one sym-
bol on that chart. That leads to developing a solution on one set of data that is 
probably too small. Further, the pointâ€andâ€click ease with which you can add 
trading rules, stops, and filters, and then optimize all of them, leads the trader 
to think â€œthis is how itâ€™s supposed to be done.â€ I say itâ€™s a conspiracy because 
the software companies know thereâ€™s a curveâ€fitting problem with that meth-
odology, but itâ€™s so easy for a potential customer to â€œfind the holy grailâ€ in a 
twoâ€week trial with this paradigm that a sale of the software is almost assured.
A better solution is to offer packages that allow the user to develop across 
a basket of symbols at the same time, but itâ€™s hard work to find a tradeable 
solution in that manner, and the spectacular trade charts you see in the ads 
wonâ€™t look nearly as good.
 
â– Other Impediments to a Valid Back-Test
If you develop a strategy and it fails in realâ€time trading, Iâ€™d guess thereâ€™s a 
90 percent probability that you curveâ€fit the strategy to the data. But there 
are other possible reasons:
 
â– Assuming every point in a bar was a trade point.
 
â– Dual orders within a bar.

25
DEVELOPING A STRATEGY SO IT TRADES LIKE IT BACKâ€TESTS
 
â– Realistically accounting for transaction costs.
 
â– Limit order mania.
The relatively good thing about these impediments is that their sever-
ity lessens as your profitâ€perâ€trade goal goes up. If you develop on daily 
bars and try to make $500, or more, per trade, one, or more, of these is-
sues might dampen your return, but they probably wonâ€™t kill the strategy. If 
youâ€™re looking to scalp $50 profits two or three times a day, any one of these 
things could kill the system and make it a net loser.
Assuming Every Point in a Bar Was a Trade Point
If you use daily bars and a stock has an open, high, low, and close for the day 
of 101, 101, 99, and 99, your backâ€testing platform will probably assume 
that there were 200 trading points that day: one at each cent of the range 
from 101 down to 99. In reality, the stock could have opened at 101 gapped 
down to 100.5 and then traded down in 1â€cent increments to the closing 
price of 99. If your strategy had a short entry at 100.95 on a stop, your soft-
ware will probably give you that price, but in reality, youâ€™d have been filled 
at 100.5, or less. If youâ€™re a longerâ€term trader, the $0.45 wonâ€™t kill you, 
but if youâ€™re trying to make $0.10 a trade, that $0.45 is four and a half trades 
of profit. The solution to this backâ€testing impediment is to go ahead and de-
velop on daily bars, but if you find something you like, do a comparison of a 
portion of your dailyâ€bar results with the results using the same strategy on 
finer resolution bars: fiveâ€minute bars, oneâ€minute bars, tick data (tick data 
is a plot of every trade point, not a bar that contains summary information 
for all trades in a given timeâ€frame), and so forth.
Dual Orders within a Bar
Many strategies actually have one or more orders valid for a single bar.  
Examples are:
 
â– Breakout systems where you place a buy stop and a sell stop before the 
market opens and let the market decide your position based on the first 
breakout.
 
â– Counterâ€trend, or trading range systems where you put a sell limit above 
the current price, and a buy limit below current price and hope that price 
moves up or down to your entry price then moves back the other way 

26
DEVELOPING A STRATEGY SO IT TRADES LIKE IT BACKâ€TESTS
and youâ€™re taken out of the trade with a profit when the other limit order 
acts as a profitâ€target.
 
â– Contingent orders where you put in an entry order and a contingent 
order for stop protection should the entry execute.
The problem with these types of orders is that thereâ€™s no way to know 
which order gets hit first if the bar range encompasses both orders. In the 
second example in this list, it doesnâ€™t matter, because both trades will always 
be winners, but in the other two it does. Again, the way to get around this 
backâ€testing issue is to go to finer resolution data to find out which order 
really got hit first.
Realistically Accounting for Transaction Costs
Transaction costs include slippage and commission. (Actually, taxes on prof-
its should probably be included, but no one talks about that.) When I re-
leased my first commercial trading system in 1993, I included transaction 
costs of $75 per contract for every 60 trading days the trade lasted. The 
60â€day addition was to account for rollover costs from one contract to the 
next. Back then a typical broker would charge you about $25 roundâ€turn 
per futures contact, and the pit would find a way to take another $50 away 
from you no matter what your order was or when you placed it (remember 
the dreaded words, â€œfast market conditionsâ€). Weâ€™ve come a long way from 
then. Brokers are much more competitive (you can trade a futures contract 
from $2 to $5 a roundâ€turn, and $25,000 worth of stock for about $5 to 
$10, inâ€andâ€out), and the electronic markets have reduced bid/ask spreads 
to 1 to a few ticks, except at night. So the impact of transaction costs on a 
trade is much less than it used to be. But if youâ€™re looking to make relatively 
small profits on a strategy, realistic transaction costs need to be factored in.
The following are the development slippage numbers I use in my backâ€
testing; you can use the actual commission numbers of your own broker.
Stocks
I only trade stocks that have an average liquidity of $20,000,000 over the last 
five trading days. I define liquidity as closing price multiplied by volume. I 
almost never use stop orders intraâ€day for liquidity considerations. I use the 
following types of orders and expect slippage to average the figure noted:
Market-on-open orders: A penalty of $0.015 from the print open; thatâ€™s 
1.5 cents.

27
DEVELOPING A STRATEGY SO IT TRADES LIKE IT BACKâ€TESTS
Market-on-close orders: A penalty of $0.0025 from the print close; thatâ€™s 
1/4 of a penny.
Limit orders: See the â€œLimit Order Maniaâ€ section that follows.
There is something you should be aware of if your account size is rela-
tively small. An odd lot is now classified as an order for less than 100 shares. 
If youâ€™re doing market-on-open orders for an odd lot, your slippage will 
average much more than an opening order for a round lot (larger than  
100 shares). Thatâ€™s because oddâ€lot orders arenâ€™t included in the opening 
pairâ€offs the exchanges do and only get filled with other matching oddâ€lot 
orders. Slippage on these can be so large, I avoid doing them.
Commodities
Iâ€™ll trade even the more illiquid commodities if the profitâ€perâ€trade for the 
strategy is relatively high, but I expect more slippage with them.
Market and stop orders during the day: $25
Market and stop exit orders during the night session: I wait until the day 
session when the spreads are better to get out.
Foreign Exchange (FX)
Iâ€™m not a big FX trader, but when I do; my orders are generally limit 
orders because I consider FX easiest to trade in a counterâ€trend manner. 
If you have a FX broker, not a marketâ€maker, and youâ€™re trading pairs that 
involve the six major currencies, three to four pips round-turn is a good 
number.
Limit Order Mania
At some point, Iâ€™m sure nearly every trader sees a potential â€œgolden 
gooseâ€ in limit orders. On the surface, they eliminate all concerns about 
slippage because you get your price, or better. But the reality is that 
slippage occurs in a different way with limit orders: missed entries. I 
used to trade a stock system that put out 20 to 30 buy limit orders each 
day. I only wanted 10 entries, so when 10 fills occurred, my software 
would cancel the rest. The system made money, but never as good as my 
backâ€tests. What was happening was that I missed some of the best fills, 
but always got filled on the ones that traded down to my price, and kept 
going down from there. In other words, I missed some big winners, but 
always seemed to get the losers.

28
DEVELOPING A STRATEGY SO IT TRADES LIKE IT BACKâ€TESTS
If price goes down to your buy limit price, you only get filled for sure if 
there is sufficient liquidity on the other side of the order to take out all the 
buy orders at that price. Otherwise you miss getting filled at all, or get only 
a partial fill. Many will argue that in the very liquid markets like the S&P 
and FX currencies there is plenty of liquidity at all price points. Thatâ€™s just 
not so. Even trading one contract, or one $100,000 FX unit, price can trade 
at your limit order and you may not get filled, but your backâ€test software 
will assume you did. Iâ€™ve sat at my screen many times with hundreds of bids 
sitting at my limit buy order price, saying, â€œCome on, give it to me,â€ and 
it doesnâ€™t happen. So the problem for strategies that use limit orders for 
entry, or as a profitâ€target exit, is that you will miss some of the fills that 
just hit your price, and your results will always be a little worse than your 
backâ€test. If your profitâ€perâ€trade is relatively large, you will probably just 
see a performance dropâ€off in your realâ€time trades versus your hypotheti-
cal trades. But if your profitâ€perâ€trade is relatively small, this problem could 
be a dealâ€breaker.
For systems that use limit orders to scalp small profits, the dropâ€off in 
real fills from hypothetical backâ€test fills can be so great that a money ma-
chine turns into a money pit. Hereâ€™s a real example. I implemented the fol-
lowing logic in â€œautoâ€tradingâ€ software on the emini S&P with a fiveâ€minute 
chart after a backâ€test showed fantastic results:
Entry Orders
Limit buy point = Close â€“ 0.75 points
Limit sell point = Close + 0.75 points
Stop after Entry
Stop = $75 from entry price (1.5 emini S&P points)
Limit Order Profit Targets
Sell to cover limit = Close + 0.75 points
Buy to cover limit = Close â€“ 0.75 points
During the oneâ€month period of March 12, 2011, to April 12, 2011, the 
strategy had these backâ€test statistics:
Wining trades: 928
Losing trades: 330
Total profit: $21,525
Profitâ€perâ€trade: $15.90
Max drawâ€down: $1,262.50

29
DEVELOPING A STRATEGY SO IT TRADES LIKE IT BACKâ€TESTS
The hypothetical equity curve was just about a straight line from the bot-
tom left to the top right of the chart, while in real trading, just about all of 
the losing trades would occur, but youâ€™d only get about half the winners. 
The only one who would marvel at the strategy would be your broker, as he 
collects over 600 roundâ€turn commissions a month.
 
â– Conclusion
After you develop a tradeable strategy, you want it to trade in realâ€time 
like it backâ€tested. This chapter illustrated the most probable reasons for a 
performance dropâ€off. By far, the likeliest reason is curveâ€fitting in the de-
velopment process. As you develop, I urge you to ask yourself the following 
question: Can I be curveâ€fitting in any way by doing this step?
Appendix D has some examples of curveâ€fitting that might surprise you. 
I urge you to look them over. The safest way to ensure a low degree of 
curveâ€fitting is to do a BRAC test after you find a strategy youâ€™re interested 
in trading.

T
his chapter explores the trading characteristics of three market classes: 
stocks, commodities, and foreign exchange (FX) currency pairs. Each 
of these classes is traded worldwide, and each has tremendous liquidity for 
ease of trading. Many traders assume that every market should be traded the 
same way, but, for whatever reason, market classes do behave differently. 
Moreover, within a market class the individual instruments behave differ-
ently in different time frames (monthly, weekly, daily, intraâ€day bars). This 
chapter will illustrate these differences and show a general approach for 
determining the characteristics of a trading class.
Finding the tendencies of a market class is a shortcut to finding good 
trading approaches for that class. If a market tends to trend in a certain time 
frame, trendâ€following techniques like moving averages, breakouts, trendâ€
lines, and so forth are a good place to start the development process. If 
a market tends to counterâ€trend behavior, overbought/oversold oscillators 
like RSI, stochastic, or momentum might be good starting points.

â– Markets Are Different: Daily Bars
To illustrate that market classes can move in fundamentally different ways, 
weâ€™ll use an easyâ€toâ€understand process on daily stock and commodity data. 
After the data is presented, youâ€™ll probably agree that stocks move primar-
ily in a counterâ€trend manner (weak stocks outperform strong stocks to 
the upside), while commodities tend to trend (strong commodities tend to 
continue upward, while weak commodities tend to continue downward). 
The process uses the following steps:
Step 1: Determine baseline buyâ€andâ€hold performance. At the start of each 
month, every member of the two classes will be invested in. For stocks, 
the equity existing at the end of the month will be divided by the num-
ber of stocks in the class and the number of shares representing that dol-
lar value will be bought. At the end of the month, those shares in each 
stock are sold at the endâ€ofâ€month prevailing price. For commodities, 
one contract of each commodity will be bought at the price on the open 
of the first trading day of the month. Those contracts will be cashed out 
at the closing price at the end of the month. For stocks, weâ€™ll use a base-
line of 1,714 highly liquid stocks from the year 2000 through the end 
of 2011. The baseline performance metric for stocks will be percent 
profitâ€perâ€trade for the month. For commodities, weâ€™ll use a basket of 
56 worldwide commodities over the same years, and the performance 
metric will be dollar return for the oneâ€lot trade.
Step 2: Determine the performance of a trendâ€following strategy on strong 
stocks and commodities. This step duplicates the buyâ€andâ€hold methodol-
ogy and metrics except the stocks and commodities that are bought at the 
first of each month are those whose closing price at the end of the month 
is one standard deviation above the average of the last 20 closes. The stan-
dard deviation measure is the standard deviation of the last 20 closes.
Step 3: Determine the performance of a counterâ€trend buy strategy on 
weak stocks and commodities. This step uses the same methodology and 
metrics as the trendâ€following strategy on strong stocks, except the in-
struments that are bought are those whose closing price is one standard 
deviation of price below the average of the last 20 closes.

You will note that this basket of stocks outperformed the general 
market over the same time frame. This is due to survivorship basis. The 
1,714 stocks were the most liquid stocks at the end of 2011 when the 
analysis was conducted. Through the years other stocks would have 
made the list, but they are no longer trading, many because of poor 
performance. Their contribution would have brought the returns in line 
with the actual market performance. Still, this is not considered a major 
flaw in the study. The same market data was presented to each of the 
three strategies.

shows the results.

This shows that instruments that have a trending tendency on one time

â– Why the Difference in Trending Tendencies 
between Classes of Instruments?
I hope this chapterâ€™s analysis has convinced you that classes of instruments 
have different trending tendencies. An obvious question is: Why? In the case 
of dailyâ€bar stocks, I believe they are primarily counterâ€trend instruments 
because they are emotionally driven rather than value driven. The emotions 
of fear and greed cause a herd reaction to movement. When stocks are ral-
lying sharply, greed causes investors to jump in so they donâ€™t miss the move. 
As more and more jump in, the valuations increase beyond fair value until 
inevitably there are no more buyers and prices head down. At that point, 
fear sets in. Profits quickly turn to losses, and the investor sells at any cost 
to just get out. When there are no more sellers, valuations are below fair 
value and an upâ€cycle starts. Though I canâ€™t prove those statements, I offer 
the following evidence:
When you buy stock you are given a piece of paper, the stock certificate, 
not a hard asset like a gold bar or bushel of grain, which forms the basis of 
value for commodities. As the dot-com bubble proved, nobody really knows 
what a company is worth. Accounting games put out the story the company 
wants to tell, which clouds transparency. Without a clear marker of worth, 
traders/investors are at a loss to knowing whether a stock is a good buy or not.
Through the stock market bubble and subsequent crash, from 1997 to 
2003, the stock market as measured by the S&P 500 futures contract had a 
daily range exceeding 1 percent of closing price 83.6 percent of the time. 
During the same time frame, our basket of 56 commodities (less the stock 
index futures) had a daily range exceeding 1 percent of price or more only 
53.6 percent of the time. The commodity currencies had a daily range of  
1 percent of price or more only 26 percent of the days during the same time 
frame. I can think of no fundamental reason why any stock market should 
move 1 percent of its value four out of every five days. I conclude that it 
must be trader emotions moving the market up and down.

FIND THE PATH OF LEAST RESISTANCE
 
â– Conclusion
Different asset classes trade differently, and within the same asset class, trad-
ing characteristics can differ by time frame. It pays to perform a little analy-
sis on what you want to trade, and in the time frame you want to trade it, 
before you start development. This chapter has illustrated an easy process 
you can use to find out the path of least resistance, which will give you a 
headâ€start in the development process.

45
T
he development of a trading strategy is an iterative process, but the 
start of the process is always the entry idea. If you are trading an 
asset class, or even a single instrument like a stock index, you need to 
have some idea how that class or instrument trades, and then test your 
ideas on how to enter the market at the appropriate time. The entry idea 
might be as simple as â€œIâ€™ll trendâ€follow by going long after two up closes, 
or short after two down closes,â€ or as complex as â€œIâ€™ll wait until I have a 
oneâ€third retracement in the third wave of an Elliott wave cycle and in-
crementally buy in a position at any further Fibonacci retracement level.â€ 
For many, the entry idea comes from the study of charts; recurring up or 
down moves seem to happen after a certain chart setup. Some entry ideas 
are stimulated by the technical analysis literature. For others, like me, test 
ideas just pop up when Iâ€™m grocery shopping or mowing the lawn. How-
ever you do it, if you find a good entry idea, the rest of the development 
process is pretty straightforward.
This chapter is the first of three that will detail the process of build-
ing a strategy. Chapter 5 explores trade exits, and Chapter 6 looks at 
trading filters that are used, primarily in conjunction with the entry, to 
screen in good trading setups or screen out bad setups. Through these 
three chapters, we will build a stock and commodity strategy to illustrate 
the process. There will be other examples where warranted. As the system 
is built, a simple money management metric will be computed from the 
equity curve that shows the worth of the latest development step. The

TRADING SYSTEM ELEMENTS: ENTRIES
process illustrated in these three chapters is how I develop systems. Youâ€™ll 
go through the process many times before you find something truly trade-
able. But even in the failures youâ€™ll learn something about the market 
youâ€™re trying to trade. Many times itâ€™s the failure that causes the break-
through. If some step makes things much worse, doing the reverse might 
just be the right thing to do.
Because of its topicâ€™s importance, this chapter will start with a process 
that allows a developer to determine how good his entry idea is. I call it de-
termining the entry power of a setup. Using the methodology, weâ€™ll quantify 
a number of popular entry techniques.
 
â– Entry: The Most Important System Element
Many will disagree with the statement that the entry is the most important 
system element. If theyâ€™re trendâ€followers, they say anyone can spot a trend 
and jump onboard, but itâ€™s when you get out that makes the difference. Sure, 
when you get out is important, but the entry determines how soon and how 
quickly you accrue profit on a trade. If youâ€™ve got a good entry, you can get 
out randomly and make a profit, on average, but you canâ€™t exit randomly and 
make a profit without a good entry, no matter how good your exit is. The 
entry is your edge, just like the houseâ€™s edge in Vegas. On average, it makes 
your trade immediately profitable; the rest of the system just determines 
when the edge has run out and itâ€™s time to exit.
Good entries have a power and persistence that are proportional to the 
number of bars used for the setup logic. Bars are information. One tells you 
little, but the more you have the better idea you should have of whatâ€™s next. 
If your entry uses 10 bars of information, it will have a forecasting power 
that builds profits to a certain level before succeeding bars either continue 
the direction, or not. If your entry uses 20 bars of information, your profit 
push should be higher and last longer than the 10â€bar entry. Similarly, with 
50 bars of information, you get a bigger profit push and longer persistence. 
Figure 4.1 illustrates the thought.
Does that mean more bars make a better signal? No. Your 10â€bar entry 
might generate enough trades in the time the 50â€bar signal generated one 
to be a better trading solution. It does mean that you can compare entry 
signals on a common basis: the number of bars used to determine the entry. 
Just take all the trades issued by each entry and accumulate the profit/loss 
on succeeding days.

 The following two entry examples illustrate entry power for the com-
modity class of tradeables:
  Trendâ€Following Entry 1: Buy when the close of the bar is above the 
nâ€day moving average. Exit the long position and go short when the 
close of the bar is below the nâ€day moving average. 
 Trendâ€Following Entry 2: Buy when the shorterâ€length nâ€day moving 
average closes above the longer n1â€day moving average. Sell when the 
shorterâ€length nâ€day moving average closes below the n1â€day moving 
average.   
 The trendâ€following Entry 1 rules summarize the first basic system 
that just about every trendâ€follower tests. It is a complete, standalone 
system because the exit of the first signal is the entry for the second. It 
is always in the market and needs no other rules. The trendâ€following 
Entry 2 rules are a more sophisticated trendâ€following strategy using 
moving averages. In the first strategy, there are always a number of 
whipsaw entries caused when no true trend is unfolding in the market-
place. The second entry system attempts to eliminate a number of these 
whipsaws by having both averages confirm the trend. Using our entry 
power methodology, Figures  4.2 and  4.3 summarize the entry power of 
the two entries.

the 20â€day entry doesnâ€™t go, and stay, positive until 10 days after entry; 
the 40â€day entry doesnâ€™t go positive until 7 days after entry; and the 
80â€day entry doesnâ€™t go, and stay, positive until 9 days after entry. If I 
was going to use one of these entries, Iâ€™d try to take advantage of this 
information. Iâ€™d look at delaying entry until the appropriate profitable 
day based on the historic averages, or look at entering at a better price 
with a limit order some measure away from the entry price of the open 
following the close. 
 This anomaly points out what you are likely to see with a number of 
common entries: Profi t doesnâ€™t start to accrue until some number of days 
after the signal. What I believe is happening in those cases is that the popu-
larity of the entry causes an abnormal number of traders to enter on those 
signals. Even though the entry may ultimately be valid, there might not 
be enough liquidity to support the abnormal number of entries and price

with the demand, and some days later the position moves into profi t. If

the price pullback.

 Relative Strength Index (RSI) 
 This indicator was introduced by J. Welles Wilder Jr. in his classic book,  New 
Concepts in Technical Trading Systems. The algorithm determines the ratio of up 
closes to down closes over a specifi ed number of bars. The output is normal-
ized to a value between 0 and 100. When used as trendâ€following methods, 
values higher than 50 are bullish, while values lower than 50 are bearish. In 
this analysis, the trendâ€following buy signal was a value of 53 or higher, and 
the trendâ€following sell signal was an RSI value of 47 or lower. Figure  4.4 
shows the results.

 Rate of Change (ROC) 
 Rate of change (ROC) is an indicator that measures the velocity of price 
change. Typically, todayâ€™s closing price is divided by the closing price some 
number of bars ago and then multiplied by 100 to yield the measurement. 
When the value is above 100, velocity is up; below 100, and velocity is 
down. Figure   4.6 shows the power of this entry signal when long trades 
are taken at an ROC of 102 or greater and short trades are taken at 98 or 
below.
 There is good separation on the lines in Figure  4.6 until about 
50 days, when things start to become muddled. The intermediate peaks 
though 50 days are less than the corresponding ones on the RSI chart 
(Figure  4.4 ).

ties you want to see: clear separation and more power in the longerâ€term 
entries. A diff erence between this set of lines and those in the RSI graphs 
is the defi nite turnâ€down in profi t some number of days after entry in the 
standard deviation graphs. 
 Donchian Entry 
 The Donchian entry was advocated by Richard Donchian in the 1960s 
for stock trading. Later, it was the baseline entry for Richard Dennis and 
William Eckhardtâ€™s Turtle system. It is a breakout system with the upper 
penetration point being the highest high, or close, of the last n bars. The 
lower penetration point is the lowest low, or close, of the last n bars. This is 
a very popular entry because of its notoriety and the fact that it works well 
in almost all time frames. Because of its popularity, almost every entry will 
have huge entry volume and corresponding slippage, or price pullback, for 
a few days. For our entry, weâ€™ll go long when price closes above the nâ€bar 
Donchian high and short when price closes below the nâ€day Donchian low 
where n is the number of bars used to determine the Donchian highs and 
lows. Figure  4.8 shows Donchian entry power performance.
 The set of lines in Figure  4.8 corresponds very closely to the standard 
deviation graph of Figure  4.7 . It is my experience that those are the best two 
wellâ€known entries.

Counterâ€Trendâ€Following Stockâ€Entry Note
Obviously the preceding analysis was not exhaustive. With an entry candi-
date, more work should be done before system development starts with that 
entry. For instance, the RSI, ROC, stochastic, and standard deviation entries 
are made at levels below normal; these would be varied. And because of 
the value of finding even moderately performing short signals, more work 
should be done on those entries that could potentially yield them. Lastly, 
remember that the graphs had a positive survivorship bias that was removed. 
In the later years that bias was much less because the stock basket is much 
closer to the 100 percent real basket that traded at the time. Iâ€™d go back and 
check performance for the last few years for interesting signals without the 
bias removal to see how good the signal really is.
Counterâ€Trendâ€Following Stock Entry  
Observations
There are clear differences between the commodity and stock graphs:
 
â– The commodity graphs have a clear separation of the nâ€day length lines 
and they vary right from the start, where the stock 10â€, 20â€, and 40â€day 
lines have very little spread. The 80â€day stock lines head straight down 
from the start.
 
â– The commodity graphs have a relatively higher average profitâ€perâ€trade 
as the number of bars increases from 10 to 80. The stock graphs gener-
ally have their worst average percent profit using 80 days of data. In most 
cases the best results use 10 bars.
 
â– Conclusion
In this chapter we explored the entry power of wellâ€known entry setups. 
The methodology was to use a large basket of a tradeable and generate entry 
signals using the setup with a spectrum of lookâ€back bars of information. 
After the entry signal, the profit was accrued across all signals out to some 
number of days and the results graphed. From the graphs, significant peak 
points can be seen and the annualized return computed. Thus entry power is 
represented graphically, and by annualized return.

64
TRADING SYSTEM ELEMENTS: ENTRIES
For commodities, the RSI, standard deviation, and Donchian entries 
looked most interesting. For stocks, the Donchian entry was far and away 
the best. But a cautionary note: The analysis was not exhaustive, only il-
lustrative. If you have an entry idea, this is the type of analysis you should 
do to find out if itâ€™s worthwhile and in what time frames it works best. In 
the next two chapters we will build a commodity and stock system on the 
Donchian entry.

Trading System 
Elements: Exits
C H A P T E R  5
T
he optimum time to exit a trade is at the peak profit point of the trade. 
Finding that point is easier said than done, and Iâ€™ve never seen anybody 
actually try to build a measure of whether todayâ€™s the peak profit day or not. 
In reality, most developers exit trades in one of four ways: through a reversal 
signal, through a stop loss, through a timeâ€based exit, or through a profit 
stop. Examples of each will be shown in this chapter using a stock strategy 
and a commodity strategy.
For this analysis, weâ€™ll use the stocks that comprised the Nasdaq 100 at 
the end of 2011 with dailyâ€bar data going back to 2000, if they traded that 
long. On the commodity side, weâ€™ll use a basket of 56 commodities with 
data from 1980 to the end of 2011. Backâ€adjusted stock and commodity 
contacts will be used, and, unless otherwise noted, transaction costs (slip-
page and commission) will not be included.
The baseline entry signal for both stocks and commodities will be the 
Donchian entry described in Chapter 4. In keeping with the trending ten-
dencies of the two classes of trading instruments, we will use a counterâ€
trend Donchian entry for stocks and a trendâ€following Donchian entry for 
commodities. For stocks, weâ€™ll initiate a $5,000 position at each long or 
short signal, and for commodities weâ€™ll buy or sell one contract.

TRADING SYSTEM ELEMENTS: EXITS
 
â– Reversal Exit Signals (Stock Example)
A reversal signal exit occurs in an alwaysâ€inâ€theâ€market system like the 
moving average system we tested in Chapter 4. The exit for the current 
position is also the entry for the reverse position. Sometimes starting the 
system development process with an entry idea and using a reversal sys-
tem to find the best parameter set for the long and short entry, while 
alternately reversing from long to short and back to long again, leads to a 
good strategy.
The trending tendency analysis in Chapter 4 indicated that a counterâ€
trend Donchian entry is a good candidate for a stock trading system entry. 
The entry/exit rules for the stock reversal system are:
Exit the short position and go long on the next open when there is a close 
below the nâ€day lookâ€back lowest close.
Exit the long position and go short on the next open when there is a close 
above the nâ€day lookâ€back highest close.
Using the 100â€stock basket, the lookâ€back period for the entry was var-
ied from 10 to 80 days, in steps of 10, to get a feel for where the best trading 
point might lie. Both long and short trades were taken. Table 5.1 shows the 
results for each lookâ€back length.

TRADING SYSTEM ELEMENTS: EXITS
Short winning trades:

Short total profit:
â€“$384,147
Short profitâ€perâ€trade:
â€“$43
Itâ€™s obvious that the short side is whatâ€™s holding this strategy back. 
On average, every short trade is a $43 loser. Some may wonder how 
the short side can have a winning percentage of 60 percent and lose 
money. This is a good example of how counterâ€trend trading usually 
works. Youâ€™re trying to buy when oversold, and cover and reverse to 
short when overbought. The range between oversold and overbought 
depends on how many days of data your setup uses. Weâ€™re using 10 days, 
so the distance between oversold and overbought is relatively small. That 
means small profits when youâ€™re right. But a stock can stay oversold all 
the way down to zero, or remain overbought all the way to the moon. 
That means large losses when youâ€™re wrong. In this case, the shortâ€side 
average winner was $258 while the average loser was $498. The fact that 
the short side performs so poorly again lines up with my development 
experience: Itâ€™s very hard to find stock strategies that perform well on 
the short side.
Based on the results, letâ€™s make the strategy a longâ€only strategy. Weâ€™ll 
still use the Donchian entry point for the short side, but instead of using 
it to close the long and enter short, the logic will be to just close out the 
long trade and wait for a new long setup. We know that the longâ€only 
strategy makes $69 per trade using the 10â€day entry. Is 10 days the best 
shortâ€term entry? Table 5.2 shows summary data when the lookâ€back pe-
riod is varied from 20 days down to two days in twoâ€day increments. 
This table introduces a gainâ€toâ€pain metric that weâ€™ll use from here on 
out. The metric is a ratio formed by dividing the average annual return 
(the gain) by the average annual max drawâ€down (the pain you have to 
experience to get your gain). The average annual max drawâ€down figure 
is found by finding the x largest drawâ€downs over the trading period and 
averaging the results. The x value is the number of years spanning the trad-
ing period. In our case, the time period is 2000 through the end of 2011. 
Thatâ€™s 12 years. The total profit is divided by 12 to form the numerator 
of the ratio, and the 12 largest drawâ€downs are found and averaged to 
form the denominator, even if more than one of the largest drawâ€downs  
occurred in the same year.

In general, as the lookâ€back is decreased from 20 days down to two, these 
are the trends:
 
â– Profitâ€perâ€trade decreases.
 
â– Annual return increases.
 
â– Average max drawâ€down decreases.
 
â– Max drawâ€down decreases.
 
â– Gainâ€toâ€pain increases.
Those are all good tendencies, except for the profitâ€perâ€trade trend. If 
using a lookâ€back of two looks like the solution we should pick, you might 
have fallen into the trap this sort of analysis can set.
The problems are the average profitâ€perâ€trade and the realities of transac-
tion costs: The average profitâ€perâ€trade is so low that transaction costs will 
substantially eat into them. If you use a low commission broker like Interac-
tive Brokers, your roundâ€turn commission for the average $5,000 position 
will probably be about $4, so your $24 a trade is down to $20 right off the 
bat. The market orders that are filled on the open for both entry and exit 
add to that toll. I believe the slippage youâ€™ll get on those orders will average 
about $0.015 per share. If the average price of a share across all entries and 
exits is $20, the 3 cents of slippage for the market orders is 0.15 percent. 
The $20 per trade weâ€™re making on each $5,000 position is 0.40 percent a 
trade. Subtracting the slippage percent from 0.40 percent, we wind up mak-
ing 0.25 percent per trade, which is $12.50 per trade on our $5,000 posi-
tions after transaction costs. Our $24 per trade number has been halved.

69
TRADING SYSTEM ELEMENTS: EXITS
My selection from Table 5.2 would be the 8â€day lookâ€back. Even with the 
$4 commission and $0.03 of slippage for the two market orders, the $62 per 
trade is only reduced to about $50.50 per trade. Thatâ€™s more than 1 percent 
of our $5,000 betâ€size. We should net about that number in real trading.
 
â– Exit Stops (Stock Example)

the trend is definitely over. I do use a catastrophic stop in almost all the com-
modity systems Iâ€™ve traded. It is the â€œuncleâ€ point of the trade. For intraâ€day 
commodity or FX systems, it might be $500 away from the entry point. For 
longerâ€term systems, it might be thousands of dollars away from the entry. If 
that point is hit, admit that the entry was bad, exit, and wait for a new setup.

I went through all three versions of the catastrophic stop for a reason. I 
think these tables illustrate the following points:
 
â– A stop loss doesnâ€™t increase your profitâ€perâ€trade. Many think that a stop elimi-
nates a large portion of the loss of the big losers and that should lead to 
an overall greater profit. They do cap the loss on the big losers, but a lot 
of trades that would have been winners or small losers will hit the stop, 
get taken out of play, and then any rebound from the stop point wonâ€™t be 
seen. The net result isnâ€™t an increase in profitâ€perâ€trade. In fact, usually 
profitâ€perâ€trade goes down. Donâ€™t think this means that I donâ€™t advocate 
stops. When they help improve the trading solution I use them. In fact, 
Iâ€™ve never traded a futures strategy without some sort of stop. On the 
other hand, Iâ€™ve never traded a stock strategy with a catastrophic stop. 
Now, if a stock strategy risked all the account equity on each trade like 
some do when trading exchange-traded funds (ETFs) like the Power-
shares QQQ (QQQ), then some sort of catastrophic stop would prob-
ably be warranted. But when youâ€™re trading a basket of stocks with a 
shortâ€term scalping strategy such as this one, the risk to the portfolio 
from each trade is just not that great. Still, it always pays to look at them 
early in the development.
 
â– A stop loss doesnâ€™t increase your total profit. Handâ€inâ€hand with the profitâ€perâ€
trade conclusion is the fact that it is rare that a catastrophic stop increases 
overall strategy profitability. Again, this doesnâ€™t mean they shouldnâ€™t be 
used. If the tradeoff between decreasing risk and decreasing profitability 
is worth it, the stop should be used.
 
â– A catastrophic stop is a riskâ€avoidance measure. As you look at the three tables 
(5.3â€“5.5) youâ€™ll see that the average max drawâ€down and max drawâ€
down numbers decrease with smaller and smaller catastrophic stops. This 
is the purpose of stops: risk control.
 
â– An inâ€theâ€market stop is not necessarily the best form of risk control. I rarely 
use inâ€theâ€market stopsâ€”not because Iâ€™m against them, but be-
cause the results of my development analysis show that the best risk/
reward solution usually lies with a stop executed at either the close 
or the next open. This doesnâ€™t even take into account the argument 
about â€œstop hunting,â€ which many traders consider a huge problem 
with inâ€theâ€market stops. Those traders believe strong hands move the 

72
TRADING SYSTEM ELEMENTS: EXITS
market to pick off resting stops for their own purposes. I donâ€™t know 
if thatâ€™s true or not, but I do know that if you have a resting stop in the 
overnight session of a commodity market, strange things can happen.
Regarding trailing stops, I made some runs on this strategy using varia-
tions of trailing stops and didnâ€™t find anything that improved the trading 
solution. Later in this chapter, weâ€™ll be developing a commodity strategy. 
Trailing stops will be detailed then. For now, letâ€™s leave our baseline stock 
strategy without a catastrophic stop.

The deficiencies of the dollarâ€based stop cause us to look at tailoring the stop 
to some measure of the recent volatility of the commodity. This has two advant-

other is nearâ€ballistic, the DAX futures index and oats are examples. Why not 
have a catastrophic stop that adjusts to the current volatility of the commodity?
There are two commonly used volatility measures: average daily range, 
or true range, and the standard deviation of closing price over some period. 
Sometimes, the entry setup also provides a measure of the recent volatility. 
That is the case with the Donchian entry. The distance between the nâ€day 
lookâ€backâ€™s high and low triggers really pinpoints the relative movement of 
the commodity over the last n days. Weâ€™ll now look at all three.

Trailing Stops
The trick to a good trailing stop on a midâ€ to longerâ€term trendâ€following 
strategy is to ratchet up the acceleration of the stop as the trade starts to go 
parabolic. Figure 5.2 shows the thought.

80
TRADING SYSTEM ELEMENTS: EXITS
 Fixed moving averages donâ€™t react fast enough as the trade starts to ac-
celerate in profi t so your open equity giveâ€back is large. In this section weâ€™ll 
look at three sets of logic that try to mimic the ideal trailing stop. The fi rst 
has been around in the open literature since 1978. 
 J. Welles Wilder Jr., in his groundbreaking book,  New Concepts in Technical 
Trading Systems,  introduced a number of innovative concepts like relative 
strength index (RSI) and average directional movement index (ADX). 
Often overlooked is his Parabolic Time/Price System. Though he used it as 
a reversal system, I think itâ€™s best used as a trailing stop. If a long trade is 
initiated, an initial stop is placed, and every day thereafter that stop moves 
up at an everâ€increasing rate, whether the position is advancing in profi t 
or not. The everâ€increasing rate is a function of the acceleration factor. In 
Wilderâ€™s book, an acceleration factor of 0.02 was used, and that number was 
increased by 0.02 every time the trade had a profi table day. But the number 
really depends on the type of trading youâ€™re doing. With shortâ€term systems 
the factor will be relatively large, and as the holding period goes longer, the 
acceleration factor needs to decrease. 
 The acceleration factor is used to tighten the stop by adding an everâ€increas-
ing increment to the existing stop. The increment is computed by multiplying 
the existing acceleration factor by the diff erence of the existing stop and the 
highâ€water point of the trade. An example will clear things up. Suppose you go 
long and your initial stop is three standard deviations below your entry price (as 
weâ€™re doing with our catastrophic commodity stop). If your entry price is 100 
and your initial stop is 85, the trade might start to play out as in Table   5.14  .  
Time
Trade Equity Buildup

The published acceleration factor of 0.02 is much too fast for this strategy. 
It cuts off over $150 of profitâ€perâ€trade and doesnâ€™t help the drawâ€down 
stats. Letâ€™s try a different trailing stop strategy.
A trailing stop I developed, and used in one of my commercially sold 
trading systems, accelerated the stop by going to shorterâ€length moving 
average trailing stops at certain profit increments. You want to start with 
a moving average thatâ€™s far enough away from the entry price so you 
donâ€™t prematurely take out a trade and then, when appreciable profit  
accrues, move to a shorterâ€length moving average. The appreciable profit  
can be any metric, but I used standard deviation. Since weâ€™re using  
20 bars of data for entry, Iâ€™ll use the standard deviation of the closes on 
the day of entry as the target. When there is a close above entry price 
plus the standard deviation level, the trailing stop moves to a shorterâ€
length moving average.

stops can be effective.

Letâ€™s try a volatilityâ€based profit target. We saw that a volatilityâ€based 
catastrophic stop worked for the commodity system because the risk was 
tailored to the recent volatility of the commodity; maybe a volatilityâ€based 
profit stop will work as well. A volatilityâ€based profit stop that Iâ€™ve used 
successfully in the past counts the number of standard deviation moves of 
profit a trade makes. When the count hits the exit number, exit is done on 
the next open. Since weâ€™re using 20 days of data for the Donchian entry, letâ€™s 
use the last 20 daysâ€™ close to compute the standard deviation. When a long 
trade is signaled, the first target is the entry point plus the one standard de-
viation value on that day. If that value is surpassed, a second target is formed 
by adding the standard deviation value on the day the target is passed to the 

84
TRADING SYSTEM ELEMENTS: EXITS
close for that day. After  x targets are hit, the trade is exited. Note that weâ€™re 
adjusting the size of each target by the prevailing standard deviation on the 
day of the adjustment, not with the standard deviation value that existed on 
the day the trade was entered. Normally, as a successful trade progresses, 
the volatility increases as the trade approaches and then goes parabolic. The 
increased volatility is accounted for in our profi t target adjustments.

â– Conclusion
In this chapter we started the development of two trading strategies, one for 
stocks and one for commodities. Both used a Donchian entry, but the stock 
entry was counterâ€trend while the commodity strategy was trendâ€following. 
To this point, weâ€™ve conditionally developed the strategies from entry 
through exit. In the next chapter, weâ€™ll look at adding filters to try and make 
the strategies tradeable.
TABLE 5.21 
(Continued)

89
C H A P T E R  6
Trading System 
Elements: Filters
I
n general, filters are used in conjunction with the entry to eliminate 
certain trades from being taken. When volatility in the S&P was high in 
the late 1990s, it was common to employ a filter to avoid going long on a 
Friday or short on a Monday. The rationale was that many traders didnâ€™t 
want to risk being long over the weekend so they exited on Friday. That 
caused the market to go down on that day more often any other day of the 
week. Conversely, the money would flow back in on Monday so it had an 
up bias. Those are trading-day-of-the-week type filters.
Other common filters are seasonal, volatility, and longerâ€term trend. 
Seasonal filters use the seasonal tendencies of an instrument to avoid trading 
against historical bias. Many traders wonâ€™t short reformulated gas going into 
the driving season, or short heating oil going into winter. For stocks, forms 
of a seasonal filter would be to avoid entering on a report day or an earnings 
day. Volatility filters are useful for keeping you out of an instrument that 
is going parabolic, or one whose trading range is so volatile that your stop 
is likely to be hit by the normal noise of its dayâ€toâ€day moves. Thereâ€™s big 
profit potential in those situations, but also abnormal risk. The longerâ€term 
trend filter is useful as an adjunct to a shorter-term system: You only take the 
trades in the direction of the longerâ€term trend.
I use filters in almost all my systems. Some are intended to enhance prof-
itability, and some are strictly meant to avoid risk. First weâ€™ll develop filters 
for the stock system; then weâ€™ll look at improving the commodity system.

Thereâ€™s one other trading-day-of-the-week type filter thatâ€™s always worth

window dressing on their portfolios at those times.
